</div>

									</div>
				
	</div>

						<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>

					<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-content-subtitle"></div></div>
					
					
					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Fruit growing on woody vines in clusters</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Grapefruit" title="Grapefruit">Grapefruit</a>.</div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the fruits of the genus Vitis. For the European grapevine, see <a href="/wiki/Vitis_vinifera" title="Vitis vinifera">Vitis vinifera</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Grape_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Grape (disambiguation)">Grape (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Abhar-iran.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Abhar-iran.JPG/170px-Abhar-iran.JPG" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Abhar-iran.JPG/255px-Abhar-iran.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Abhar-iran.JPG/340px-Abhar-iran.JPG 2x" data-file-width="648" data-file-height="864" /></a><figcaption>Grapes</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Grapes,_Rostov-on-Don,_Russia.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Grapes%2C_Rostov-on-Don%2C_Russia.jpg/220px-Grapes%2C_Rostov-on-Don%2C_Russia.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Grapes%2C_Rostov-on-Don%2C_Russia.jpg/330px-Grapes%2C_Rostov-on-Don%2C_Russia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Grapes%2C_Rostov-on-Don%2C_Russia.jpg/440px-Grapes%2C_Rostov-on-Don%2C_Russia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4032" data-file-height="2688" /></a><figcaption>"Black" (dark blue) and "white" (light green) <a href="/wiki/Table_grape" title="Table grape">table grapes</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>A <b>grape</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Botany" title="Botany">botanically</a> a <a href="/wiki/Berry_(botany)" title="Berry (botany)">berry</a>, of the <a href="/wiki/Deciduous" title="Deciduous">deciduous</a> woody <a href="/wiki/Vine" title="Vine">vines</a> of the flowering plant genus <i><a href="/wiki/Vitis" title="Vitis">Vitis</a></i>. Grapes are a non-<a href="/wiki/Climacteric_(botany)" title="Climacteric (botany)">climacteric</a> type of fruit, generally occurring in clusters.
</p><p>The cultivation of grapes began perhaps 8,000 years ago, and the fruit has been used as human food over history. Eaten fresh or in dried form (as <a href="/wiki/Raisin" title="Raisin">raisins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zante_currant" title="Zante currant">currants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sultana_(grape)" title="Sultana (grape)">sultanas</a>), grapes also hold cultural significance in many parts of the world, particularly for their role in <a href="/wiki/Winemaking" title="Winemaking">winemaking</a>. Other grape-derived products include various types of jam, juice, vinegar and oil.
</p>
<div style="clear:left;" class=""></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1218072481">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-header,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data div{background:#1f1f23!important;color:#f8f9fa}@media(prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data div{background:#1f1f23!important;color:#f8f9fa}}</style><table class="infobox nowrap"><caption class="infobox-title" style="white-space:normal; <meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> is generally described as the homeland of grapes and the cultivation of this plant began there 6,000–8,000 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-This_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-This-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Yeast_(wine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Yeast (wine)">Yeast</a>, one of the earliest domesticated <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a>, occurs naturally on the skins of grapes, leading to the discovery of alcoholic drinks such as wine. The earliest archeological evidence for a dominant position of wine-making in human culture dates from 8,000 years ago in <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Areni-1_winery" title="Areni-1 winery">oldest known winery</a> was found in <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a>, dating to around 4000 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> By the 9th century AD, the city of <a href="/wiki/Shiraz" title="Shiraz">Shiraz</a> was known to produce some of the finest wines in the Middle East. Thus it has been proposed that <a href="/wiki/Syrah" title="Syrah">Syrah</a> red wine is named after <a href="/wiki/Shiraz" title="Shiraz">Shiraz</a>, a city in Persia where the grape was used to make <a href="/wiki/Shirazi_wine" class="mw-redirect" title="Shirazi wine">Shirazi wine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hugh_J_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hugh_J-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egyptian</a> <a href="/wiki/Hieroglyphics" class="mw-redirect" title="Hieroglyphics">hieroglyphics</a> record the cultivation of purple grapes, and history attests to the ancient <a href="/wiki/Greeks_(wine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Greeks (wine)">Greeks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cypriot_wine" title="Cypriot wine"> Cypriots</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phoenicians_(wine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Phoenicians (wine)">Phoenicians</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome_(wine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Rome (wine)">Romans</a> growing purple grapes both for eating and wine production.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The growing of grapes would later spread to other regions in Europe, as well as North Africa, and eventually in <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>.
</p><p>In 2005 a team of archaeologists concluded that some <a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">Chalcolithic</a> wine jars, which were discovered in Cyprus in the 1930s, were the oldest of their kind in the world, dating back to 3,500 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Moreover, Commandaria, a sweet dessert wine from Cyprus, is the oldest manufactured wine in the world, its origins traced as far back as 2000 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>In North America, native grapes belonging to various species of the genus <i>Vitis</i> proliferate in the wild across the continent, and were a part of the diet of many <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Native Americans</a>, but were considered by early European colonists to be unsuitable for wine. In the 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Ephraim_Bull" class="mw-redirect" title="Ephraim Bull">Ephraim Bull</a> of <a href="/wiki/Concord,_Massachusetts" title="Concord, Massachusetts">Concord, Massachusetts</a>, cultivated seeds from wild <i><a href="/wiki/Vitis_labrusca" title="Vitis labrusca">Vitis labrusca</a></i> vines to create the <a href="/wiki/Concord_grape" title="Concord grape">Concord grape</a> which would become an important agricultural crop in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-Robinson_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Robinson-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span></h2>
<p>Grapes are a type of <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruit</a> that grow in clusters of 15 to 300, and can be crimson, black, dark blue, yellow, green, orange, and pink. "White" grapes are actually green in color, and are evolutionarily derived from the purple grape. <a href="/wiki/Mutation" title="Mutation">Mutations</a> in two <a href="/wiki/Regulatory_genes" class="mw-redirect" title="Regulatory genes">regulatory genes</a> of white grapes turn off production of <a href="/wiki/Anthocyanin" title="Anthocyanin">anthocyanins</a>, which are responsible for the color of purple grapes.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Anthocyanins and other <a href="/wiki/Pigment" title="Pigment">pigment</a> chemicals of the larger family of <a href="/wiki/Polyphenols_in_grape" class="mw-redirect" title="Polyphenols in grape">polyphenols</a> in purple grapes are responsible for the varying shades of purple in red wines.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Grapes are typically an ellipsoid shape resembling a <a href="/wiki/Prolate_spheroid" class="mw-redirect" title="Prolate spheroid">prolate spheroid</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutrition">Nutrition</span></h3>
<p>Raw grapes are 81% water, 18% <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, 1% <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a>, and have negligible <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a> (table). A <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1154941027">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);clip-path:polygon(0px 0px,0px 0px,0px 0px);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style>100-gram (<span class="frac">3<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">2</span></span>-ounce) reference amount of raw grapes supplies 288 kilojoules (69 kilocalories) of <a href="/wiki/Food_energy" title="Food energy">food energy</a> and a moderate amount of <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_K" title="Vitamin K">vitamin K</a> (14% of the <a href="/wiki/Daily_Value" class="mw-redirect" title="Daily Value">Daily Value</a>), with no other <a href="/wiki/Micronutrient" title="Micronutrient">micronutrients</a> in significant amounts.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grapevines">Grapevines</span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Vitis" title="Vitis">Vitis</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:ConcordGrapes.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/ConcordGrapes.jpg/170px-ConcordGrapes.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="182" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/ConcordGrapes.jpg/255px-ConcordGrapes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/ConcordGrapes.jpg/340px-ConcordGrapes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="659" data-file-height="705" /></a><figcaption>Concord is a variety of North American <i>labrusca</i> grape</figcaption></figure>
<p>Most domesticated grapes come from <a href="/wiki/List_of_grape_varieties" title="List of grape varieties">cultivars</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Vitis_vinifera" title="Vitis vinifera">Vitis vinifera</a></i>, a grapevine native to the Mediterranean and Central Asia. Minor amounts of fruit and wine come from American and Asian species such as:
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Vitis_amurensis" title="Vitis amurensis">Vitis amurensis</a></i>, the most important Asian species</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Vitis_labrusca" title="Vitis labrusca">Vitis labrusca</a></i>, the North American table and grape juice grapevines (including the <a href="/wiki/Concord_(grape)" class="mw-redirect" title="Concord (grape)">Concord</a> <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivar</a>), sometimes used for wine, are native to the Eastern United States and Canada.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Vitis_mustangensis" title="Vitis mustangensis">Vitis mustangensis</a></i> (the mustang grape), found in Mississippi, Alabama, Louisiana, Texas, and Oklahoma</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Vitis_riparia" title="Vitis riparia">Vitis riparia</a></i>, a wild vine of North America, is sometimes used for winemaking and for jam. It is native to the entire Eastern United States and north to <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Vitis_rotundifolia" title="Vitis rotundifolia">Vitis rotundifolia</a></i> (the muscadine), used for jams and wine, is native to the Southeastern United States from <a href="/wiki/Delaware" title="Delaware">Delaware</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a>.</li></ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_production">Distribution and production</span></h2>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-center" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Top_grapes_countries_producers_in_the_world.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Top_grapes_countries_producers_in_the_world.png/700px-Top_grapes_countries_producers_in_the_world.png" decoding="async" width="700" height="307" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Top_grapes_countries_producers_in_the_world.png/1050px-Top_grapes_countries_producers_in_the_world.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Top_grapes_countries_producers_in_the_world.png/1400px-Top_grapes_countries_producers_in_the_world.png 2x" data-file-width="1425" data-file-height="625" /></a><figcaption>Top 20 grape producing countries in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a> (FAO), 75,866 square kilometers of the world are dedicated to grapes. Approximately 71% of world grape production is used for wine, 27% as fresh fruit, and 2% as <a href="/wiki/Dried_fruit" title="Dried fruit">dried fruit</a>. A portion of grape production goes to producing grape juice to be reconstituted for fruits canned "with no <a href="/wiki/Added_sugar" title="Added sugar">added sugar</a>" and "100% natural". The area dedicated to vineyards is increasing by about 2% per year.
</p><p>There are no reliable statistics that break down grape production by variety. It is believed that the most widely planted variety is <a href="/wiki/Sultana_(grape)" title="Sultana (grape)">Sultana</a>, also known as Thompson Seedless, with at least 3,600&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (880,000 acres) dedicated to it. The second most common variety is <a href="/wiki/Air%C3%A9n" title="Airén">Airén</a>. Other popular varieties include <a href="/wiki/Cabernet_Sauvignon" title="Cabernet Sauvignon">Cabernet Sauvignon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sauvignon_blanc" title="Sauvignon blanc">Sauvignon blanc</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cabernet_Franc" title="Cabernet Franc">Cabernet Franc</a>, <a href="/wiki/Merlot" title="Merlot">Merlot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grenache" title="Grenache">Grenache</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tempranillo" title="Tempranillo">Tempranillo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Riesling" title="Riesling">Riesling</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chardonnay" title="Chardonnay">Chardonnay</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Table_and_wine_grapes">Table and wine grapes</span></h2>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Wine_grapes03.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Wine_grapes03.jpg/170px-Wine_grapes03.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="257" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Wine_grapes03.jpg/255px-Wine_grapes03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Wine_grapes03.jpg/340px-Wine_grapes03.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1060" data-file-height="1600" /></a><figcaption>Wine grapes on the vine</figcaption></figure>
<p>Commercially cultivated grapes can usually be classified as either <a href="/wiki/Table_grape" title="Table grape">table</a> or wine grapes, based on their intended method of consumption: eaten raw (table grapes) or used to make <a href="/wiki/Wine" title="Wine">wine</a> (wine grapes). The sweetness of grapes depends on when they are harvested, as they do not continue to ripen once picked.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> While almost all of them belong to the same species, <i><a href="/wiki/Vitis_vinifera" title="Vitis vinifera">Vitis vinifera</a></i>, table and wine grapes have significant differences, brought about through <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">selective breeding</a>. Table grape cultivars tend to have large, seedless fruit (see below) with relatively thin skin. Wine grapes are smaller, usually seeded, and have relatively thick skins (a desirable characteristic in winemaking, since much of the aroma in wine comes from the skin). Wine grapes also tend to be very sweet: they are harvested at the time when their juice is approximately 24% sugar by weight. By comparison, commercially produced "100% grape juice", made from table grapes, is usually around 15% sugar by weight.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Seedless_grapes">Seedless grapes</span></h2>
<p>Seedless cultivars now make up the overwhelming majority of table grape plantings. Because grapevines are <a href="/wiki/Vegetative_reproduction" title="Vegetative reproduction">vegetatively propagated</a> by cuttings, the lack of seeds does not present a problem for reproduction. It is an issue for breeders, who must either use a seeded variety as the female parent or rescue embryos early in development using <a href="/wiki/Plant_tissue_culture" title="Plant tissue culture">tissue culture</a> techniques.
</p><p>There are several sources of the seedlessness trait, and essentially all commercial cultivators get it from one of three sources: <a href="/wiki/Thompson_Seedless" class="mw-redirect" title="Thompson Seedless">Thompson Seedless</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_Seedless&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Russian Seedless (page does not exist)">Russian Seedless</a>, and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_Monukka&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Black Monukka (page does not exist)">Black Monukka</a>, all being cultivars of <i><a href="/wiki/Vitis_vinifera" title="Vitis vinifera">Vitis vinifera</a></i>. There are currently more than a dozen varieties of seedless grapes. Several, such as Einset Seedless, Benjamin Gunnels's Prime seedless grapes, Reliance, and Venus, have been specifically cultivated for hardiness and quality in the relatively cold climates of northeastern United States and southern <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>An offset to the improved eating quality of seedlessness is the loss of potential health benefits provided by the enriched <a href="/wiki/Phytochemical" title="Phytochemical">phytochemical</a> content of grape seeds (see <a href="#Health_claims">Health claims</a>, below).<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Culinary">Culinary</span></h3>
<p>Grapes are eaten raw, dried (as raisins, currants and sultanas), or cooked. Also, depending on grape cultivar, grapes are used in winemaking. Grapes can be processed into a multitude of products such as jams, juices, vinegars and oils.
Commercially cultivated grapes are classified as either table or wine grapes. These categories are based on their intended method of consumption: grapes that are eaten raw (table grapes), or grapes that are used to make wine (wine grapes).
Table grape cultivars normally have large, seedless fruit and thin skins. Wine grapes are smaller (in comparison to table grapes), usually contains seeds, and have thicker skins (a desirable characteristic in making wine. Most of the aroma in wine is from the skin. Wine grapes tend to have a high sugar content. They are harvested at peak sugar levels (approximately 24% sugar by weight.) In comparison, commercially produced "100% grape juice" made from table grapes are normally around 15% sugar by weight.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Raisins.2C_currants_and_sultanas"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Raisins,_currants_and_sultanas">Raisins, currants and sultanas</span></h4>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Raisin" title="Raisin">Raisin</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Raisins_01.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Raisins_01.jpg/220px-Raisins_01.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="138" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Raisins_01.jpg/330px-Raisins_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Raisins_01.jpg/440px-Raisins_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3223" data-file-height="2025" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Raisin" title="Raisin">Raisins</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>In most of Europe and North America, dried grapes are referred to as "raisins" or the local equivalent. In the UK, three different varieties are recognized, forcing the EU to use the term "dried vine fruit" in official documents.
</p><p>A <i><a href="/wiki/Raisin" title="Raisin">raisin</a></i> is any dried grape. While <i>raisin</i> is a French <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">loanword</a>, the word in French refers to the fresh fruit; <i>grappe</i> (from which the English <i>grape</i> is derived) refers to the bunch (as in <i>une grappe de raisins</i>). A raisin in French is called <i>raisin sec</i> ("dry grape").
</p><p>A <i><a href="/wiki/Zante_currant" title="Zante currant">currant</a></i> is a dried <a href="/wiki/Zakynthos" title="Zakynthos">Zante</a> Black Corinth grape, the name being a corruption of the French <i>raisin de Corinthe</i> (<a href="/wiki/Corinth" title="Corinth">Corinth</a> grape). The names of the black and red currant, now more usually <a href="/wiki/Blackcurrant" title="Blackcurrant">blackcurrant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Redcurrant" title="Redcurrant">redcurrant</a>, two berries unrelated to grapes, are derived from this use. Some other fruits of similar appearance are also so named, for example, Australian currant, native currant, Indian currant.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A <i>sultana</i> was originally a raisin made from <a href="/wiki/Sultana_(grape)" title="Sultana (grape)">Sultana grapes</a> of Turkish origin (known as Thompson Seedless in the United States), but the word is now applied to raisins made from either white grapes or red grapes that are bleached to resemble the traditional sultana.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Juice">Juice</span></h4>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Grape_Juice.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Grape_Juice.jpg/170px-Grape_Juice.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="255" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Grape_Juice.jpg/255px-Grape_Juice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Grape_Juice.jpg/340px-Grape_Juice.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1334" data-file-height="2000" /></a><figcaption>Grape juice</figcaption></figure>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Grape_juice" title="Grape juice">Grape juice</a></div>
<p>Grape juice is obtained from crushing and blending grapes into a liquid. The juice is often sold in stores or <a href="/wiki/Fermentation_(wine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fermentation (wine)">fermented</a> and made into <a href="/wiki/Wine" title="Wine">wine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brandy" title="Brandy">brandy</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Vinegar" title="Vinegar">vinegar</a>.  Grape juice that has been pasteurized, removing any naturally occurring yeast, will not ferment if kept sterile, and thus contains no alcohol.  In the <a href="/wiki/Wine" title="Wine">wine</a> industry, grape juice that contains 7–23% of pulp, skins, stems and seeds is often referred to as "<a href="/wiki/Must" title="Must">must</a>". In North America, the most common grape juice is purple and made from <a href="/wiki/Concord_(grape)" class="mw-redirect" title="Concord (grape)">Concord grapes</a>, while white grape juice is commonly made from <a href="/wiki/Niagara_(grape)" title="Niagara (grape)">Niagara grapes</a>, both of which are varieties of native American grapes, a different species from European wine grapes. In California, Sultana (known there as Thompson Seedless) grapes are sometimes diverted from the raisin or table market to produce white juice.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pomace_and_phytochemicals">Pomace and phytochemicals</span></h2>
<p>Winemaking from red and white grape flesh and skins produces substantial quantities of organic residues, collectively called <a href="/wiki/Pomace" title="Pomace">pomace</a> (also "marc"), which includes crushed skins, seeds, stems, and leaves generally used as <a href="/wiki/Compost" title="Compost">compost</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-gomez_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gomez-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Grape pomace &#8211; some 10-30% of the total mass of grapes crushed &#8211; contains various <a href="/wiki/Phytochemical" title="Phytochemical">phytochemicals</a>, such as unfermented sugars, alcohol, <a href="/wiki/Polyphenol" title="Polyphenol">polyphenols</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tannin" title="Tannin">tannins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anthocyanin" title="Anthocyanin">anthocyanins</a>, and numerous other compounds, some of which are harvested and <a href="/wiki/Extract" title="Extract">extracted</a> for commercial applications (a process sometimes called "valorization" of the pomace).<sup id="cite_ref-gomez_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gomez-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Muhlack_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Muhlack-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Skin">Skin</span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Wine_grape_diagram_en.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Anatomical-style diagram of three grapes on their stalks. Two of the grapes are shown in cross-section with all their internal parts labeled." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Wine_grape_diagram_en.svg/300px-Wine_grape_diagram_en.svg.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="259" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Wine_grape_diagram_en.svg/450px-Wine_grape_diagram_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Wine_grape_diagram_en.svg/600px-Wine_grape_diagram_en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="678" data-file-height="585" /></a><figcaption>Grape cross-section</figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Anthocyanin" title="Anthocyanin">Anthocyanins</a> tend to be the main <a href="/wiki/Polyphenols_in_grape" class="mw-redirect" title="Polyphenols in grape">polyphenolics</a> in purple grapes, whereas <a href="/wiki/Flavan-3-ol" title="Flavan-3-ol">flavan-3-ols</a> (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Catechin" title="Catechin">catechins</a>) are the more abundant class of polyphenols in white varieties.<sup id="cite_ref-hozczp_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hozczp-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Total phenolic content is higher in purple varieties due almost entirely to anthocyanin density in purple grape skin compared to absence of anthocyanins in white grape skin.<sup id="cite_ref-hozczp_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hozczp-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Phenolic content of grape skin varies with <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivar</a>, soil composition, climate, geographic origin, and cultivation practices or exposure to diseases, such as fungal infections.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Muscadine" class="mw-redirect" title="Muscadine">Muscadine</a> grapes contain a relatively high phenolic content among dark grapes.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Xu_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Xu-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> In muscadine skins, <a href="/wiki/Ellagic_acid" title="Ellagic acid">ellagic acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myricetin" title="Myricetin">myricetin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quercetin" title="Quercetin">quercetin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kaempferol" title="Kaempferol">kaempferol</a>, and trans-resveratrol are major phenolics.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The flavonols <a href="/wiki/Syringetin" title="Syringetin">syringetin</a>, syringetin 3-O-galactoside, <a href="/wiki/Laricitrin" title="Laricitrin">laricitrin</a> and laricitrin 3-O-galactoside are also found in purple grape but absent in white grape.<sup id="cite_ref-Mattivi_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mattivi-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Seeds">Seeds</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Grape_seed_extract" title="Grape seed extract">Grape seed extract</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grape_seed_oil" title="Grape seed oil">Grape seed oil</a></div>
<p>Muscadine grape seeds contain about twice the total polyphenol content of skins.<sup id="cite_ref-Xu_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Xu-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Grape_seed_oil" title="Grape seed oil">Grape seed oil</a> from crushed seeds is used in <a href="/wiki/Cosmeceutical" title="Cosmeceutical">cosmeceuticals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Skincare" class="mw-redirect" title="Skincare">skincare</a> products. Grape seed oil, including <a href="/wiki/Tocopherol" title="Tocopherol">tocopherols</a> (<a href="/wiki/Vitamin_E" title="Vitamin E">vitamin E</a>) and high contents of <a href="/wiki/Phytosterol" title="Phytosterol">phytosterols</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polyunsaturated_fatty_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="Polyunsaturated fatty acid">polyunsaturated fatty acids</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Linoleic_acid" title="Linoleic acid">linoleic acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oleic_acid" title="Oleic acid">oleic acid</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alpha-linolenic_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="Alpha-linolenic acid">alpha-linolenic acid</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Resveratrol">Resveratrol</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Resveratrol" title="Resveratrol">Resveratrol</a></div>
<p>Resveratrol, a <a href="/wiki/Stilbene" title="Stilbene">stilbene</a> compound, is found in widely varying amounts among grape varieties, primarily in their skins and seeds.<sup id="cite_ref-lpi_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Muscadine" class="mw-redirect" title="Muscadine">Muscadine</a> grapes have about one hundred times higher concentration of stilbenes than pulp. Fresh grape skin contains about 50 to 100 micrograms of resveratrol per gram.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_claims">Health claims</span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Health_effects_of_wine" title="Health effects of wine">Health effects of wine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grape_therapy" title="Grape therapy">Grape therapy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Grape_and_raisin_toxicity_in_dogs" class="mw-redirect" title="Grape and raisin toxicity in dogs">Grape and raisin toxicity in dogs</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="French_paradox">French paradox</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/French_paradox" title="French paradox">French paradox</a></div>
<p>Comparing diets among Western countries, researchers have discovered that although French people tend to eat higher levels of animal fat, the incidence of <a href="/wiki/Heart_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Heart disease">heart disease</a> remains low in France. This phenomenon has been termed the <a href="/wiki/French_paradox" title="French paradox">French paradox</a>, and is thought to occur from protective benefits of regularly consuming red wine, among other dietary practices. Alcohol consumption in moderation may be cardioprotective by its minor <a href="/wiki/Anticoagulant" title="Anticoagulant">anticoagulant</a> effect and <a href="/wiki/Vasodilation" title="Vasodilation">vasodilation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Armenian_dolma.jpeg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Armenian_dolma.jpeg/200px-Armenian_dolma.jpeg" decoding="async" width="200" height="267" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Armenian_dolma.jpeg/300px-Armenian_dolma.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Armenian_dolma.jpeg/400px-Armenian_dolma.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="800" /></a><figcaption>Using grape leaves in cuisine (<a href="/wiki/Dolma" title="Dolma">Dolma</a>)</figcaption></figure>
<p>Although adoption of wine consumption is generally not recommended by health authorities,<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> some research indicates moderate consumption, such as one glass of red wine a day for women and two for men, may confer health benefits.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Alcohol itself may have protective effects on the cardiovascular system.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Grape_and_raisin_toxicity_in_dogs">Grape and raisin toxicity in dogs</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Grape_and_raisin_toxicity_in_dogs" class="mw-redirect" title="Grape and raisin toxicity in dogs">Grape and raisin toxicity in dogs</a></div>
<p>The consumption of grapes and raisins presents a potential health threat to dogs. Their toxicity to dogs can cause the animal to develop <a href="/wiki/Acute_kidney_failure" class="mw-redirect" title="Acute kidney failure">acute kidney failure</a> (the sudden development of kidney failure) with <a href="/wiki/Anuria" title="Anuria">anuria</a> (a lack of urine production) and may be fatal.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_religion">In religion</span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Christian_views_on_alcohol" title="Christian views on alcohol">Christian views on alcohol</a></div>
<p>Christians have traditionally used wine during worship services as a means of remembering the <a href="/wiki/Blood_of_Christ" title="Blood of Christ">blood of Jesus Christ</a> which was <a href="/wiki/Atonement_in_Christianity" class="mw-redirect" title="Atonement in Christianity">shed for the remission of sins</a>. Christians who oppose the partaking of alcoholic beverages sometimes use grape juice as the "cup" or "wine" in the <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">Lord's Supper</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-UMC_–_Grape_Juice_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UMC_–_Grape_Juice-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> continues to use wine in the celebration of the <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">Eucharist</a> because it is part of the tradition passed down through the ages starting with Jesus Christ at the <a href="/wiki/Last_Supper" title="Last Supper">Last Supper</a>, where Catholics believe the consecrated bread and wine become the <a href="/wiki/Body_of_Christ" title="Body of Christ">body</a> and blood of Jesus Christ, a dogma known as <a href="/wiki/Transubstantiation" title="Transubstantiation">transubstantiation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Wine is used (not grape juice) both due to its strong Scriptural roots, and also to follow the tradition set by the early Christian Church.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/1983_Code_of_Canon_Law" title="1983 Code of Canon Law">Code of Canon Law of the Catholic Church</a> (1983), Canon 924 says that the wine used must be natural, made from grapes of the vine, and not corrupt.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1215172403"><cite class="citation web cs1"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://iranicaonline.org/articles/angur-grapes">"ANGŪR – Encyclopaedia Iranica"</a>. <i>iranicaonline.org</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2021-04-06</span></span>. <q>According to A. de Candolle (L'Origine des plantes cultivées, Paris, 5th ed., 1912, p. 152) the grape-vine is at home in the region south of the Caucasus, from the Black Sea to the Caspian region of Iran, where "it has the shape of a strong liana climbing over high trees and producing abundant fruit without any pruning or cultivation." His statement is still generally accepted, since the greatest diversity in varieties can be observed there.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=iranicaonline.org&amp;rft.atitle=ANG%C5%AAR+%E2%80%93+Encyclopaedia+Iranica&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Firanicaonline.org%2Farticles%2Fangur-grapes&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGrape" class="Z3988"></span></span>
